  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer12224123776670563700">Years after his escape, posing as scholarly Dr. Fell, curator of a grand family's palazzo, Hannibal lives the good life in Florence, playing lovely tunes by serial killer/composer Henry VIII and killing hardly anyone himself. Clarice is unluckier: in the novel's action-film-like opening scene, she survives an FBI shootout gone wrong, and her nemesis, Paul Krendler, makes h</span>
  <span id="freeText12224123776670563700" style="display:none">Years after his escape, posing as scholarly Dr. Fell, curator of a grand family's palazzo, Hannibal lives the good life in Florence, playing lovely tunes by serial killer/composer Henry VIII and killing hardly anyone himself. Clarice is unluckier: in the novel's action-film-like opening scene, she survives an FBI shootout gone wrong, and her nemesis, Paul Krendler, makes her the fall guy. Clarice is suspended, so, unfortunately, the first cop who stumbles on Hannibal is an Italian named Pazzi, who takes after his ancestors, greedy betrayers depicted in Dante's Inferno. Pazzi is on the take from a character as scary as Hannibal: Mason Verger. When Verger was a young man busted for raping children, his vast wealth saved him from jail. All he needed was psychotherapy--with Dr. Lecter. Thanks to the treatment, Verger is now on a respirator, paralyzed except for one crablike hand, watching his enormous, brutal moray eel swim figure eights and devour fish. His obsession is to feed Lecter to some other brutal pets.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12224123776670563700" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {

              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16828545697837652005"><b>Hannibal (Hannibal Lecter #3), Thomas Harris</b><br/><br/>Hannibal is a novel by American author Thomas Harris, published in 1999. It is the third in his series featuring Dr. <br/><br/>Hannibal Lecter and the second to feature FBI Special Agent Clarice Starling. The novel takes place seven years after the events of The Silence of the Lambs and deals with the intended revenge of one of Lecter's victims. It was adapted as a film of the same name in 2001, directed by Ridley Scott. <br/><br/>عنوانها: «هانیبال»؛ «ادامه ی سکوت بره ها»</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_4939679" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4939679" />
    <a title="Peggy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/275001-peggy"><img alt="Peggy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1187021327p2/275001.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4939679?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peggy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peggy" href="/user/show/275001-peggy">Peggy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4939679" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer854806006559340714">Okay, let me confess up front: I loved <i>Red Dragon</i> and <i>The Silence of the Lambs</i>. <b>Loved</b> them. I enjoyed the movies, too: the movie version of <i>Silence of the Lambs</i> scared the pee out of me, and even so, I didn’t want it to end. So, long years later when I finally got hold of a copy of <i>Hannibal</i>, I really, really, wanted to love it, too.<br/> <br/>But I didn’t.<br/><br/>Well, that’s not entirely true. If I pretend that this wasn’t a sequel about characters I already know, then I can find some bright spots. The book has </span>
  <span id="freeText854806006559340714" style="display:none">Okay, let me confess up front: I loved <i>Red Dragon</i> and <i>The Silence of the Lambs</i>. <b>Loved</b> them. I enjoyed the movies, too: the movie version of <i>Silence of the Lambs</i> scared the pee out of me, and even so, I didn’t want it to end. So, long years later when I finally got hold of a copy of <i>Hannibal</i>, I really, really, wanted to love it, too.<br /> <br />But I didn’t.<br /><br />Well, that’s not entirely true. If I pretend that this wasn’t a sequel about characters I already know, then I can find some bright spots. The book has some fantastic descriptions of Italy. There are certainly some creepy scenes that gave me the shivers. I was fascinated by the concept of the memory cathedral. And I felt terribly bad for poor Clarice as her world crumbled in around her. The problem is, none of the characters seem remotely connected to the folks we met before.<br /><br />Hannibal Lecter, an enigma in previous installments, now has a background. It’s tragic and horrifying, but is it enough to form the Hannibal we all know? Maybe. But even if it is, do we really have to know the details of why Lecter is who he is? I’m not convinced that this information makes him a more compelling character.<br /><br />Clarice Starling, whose wagon was hitched to a rising star at the end of Silence, is on the verge of being pushed out of the FBI. She has been overlooked again and again for promotion and is reduced to being scapegoated by talentless superiors.<br /><br />Jack Crawford, a hero and mentor in the previous books, is now (for the short time he appears here) a liability.<br /><br />I understand that people change, but come on. So what happened? What changed between the publication of <i>The Silence of the Lambs</i> and the publication of <i>Hannibal</i>? Well, what changed was the character of Hannibal Lecter. In both <i>Red Dragon</i> and <i>The Silence of the Lambs</i>, Hannibal was a minor (though quite compelling) character. Harris went to great pains to point out that, although clever and extremely cunning, Lecter was not omniscient. There was always an explanation as to how he knew the things he did, and Crawford was equally clever at figuring it out. Enter Jonathan Demme.<br /> <br />When Jonathan Demme made the movie version of <i>Silence</i>, he said that he wanted the audience to believe that Lecter was the smartest man alive. It didn’t matter how he knew the things he did—he just <b>knew</b>. And to the credit of both Demme and Anthony Hopkins, it worked. The movie firmly established Lecter’s genius, and in the context of the film, it was brilliant: you never have to explain how Lecter gets his information, and his outrageous escape becomes plausible. Besides, the smarter Lecter is the more the audience worries about Clarice. Hopkins’ performance firmly established a picture in our minds of who Lecter was and how he worked.<br /><br />Enter Thomas Harris, trying to write a sequel to a phenomenally popular book, which was also a hugely successful movie. Now everyone thought of Anthony Hopkins when they thought of Hannibal Lecter, and they believed he was the smartest psycho alive. Instead of writing about his own Hannibal, he tried to write about the Demme/Hopkins Hannibal, and that just didn’t leave him anyplace to go but over the top, which is a crying shame. The book collapses under the sheer magnitude of what we are expected to accept about these characters and where they end up. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="854806006559340714" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2847391281" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2847391281" />
    <a title="Matthew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16254355-matthew"><img alt="Matthew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464987365p2/16254355.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2847391281?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew" href="/user/show/16254355-matthew">Matthew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781415951415" href="/book/show/17304398-hannibal">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=suspense">suspense</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2847391281" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17559899830564871513">It has been many years since I read a Hannibal Lecter book. The last Thomas Harris book I read was a couple of years ago, Black Sunday, and is very different from his Lecter titles. I noticed some of my friends were doing a buddy read of Hannibal, so I figured it was about time to give it a try<br/><br/>I will say 3 to 3.5 stars.<br/><br/>I liked it. But, it was a fifty/fifty thing. Half of it was the gruesome, suspenseful, mysterious story that I remember from previous Lecter books and movies. The other half was d</span>
  <span id="freeText17559899830564871513" style="display:none">It has been many years since I read a Hannibal Lecter book. The last Thomas Harris book I read was a couple of years ago, Black Sunday, and is very different from his Lecter titles. I noticed some of my friends were doing a buddy read of Hannibal, so I figured it was about time to give it a try<br /><br />I will say 3 to 3.5 stars.<br /><br />I liked it. But, it was a fifty/fifty thing. Half of it was the gruesome, suspenseful, mysterious story that I remember from previous Lecter books and movies. The other half was drawn out, slow, and sometimes uncomfortable weirdness. I think that some people might enjoy those parts, but to me they just felt like filler. And, the uncomfortable weirdness was in the few spots where it went from believable insanity to silly WTF-ness. A big part of this for me was the end - it just got way too out there to be satisfying.<br /><br />Overall, what it felt like to me was like Harris did so well with Red Dragon and Silence of the Lambs, that his publisher convinced him to write the next chapter in Lecter story and he just didn't have enough for a full novel. So, in order to get a complete book, the came up with some bizarre stuff to fluff it up a bit. I don't think that everyone will feel this way about it, but I couldn't avoid feeling that way no matter how much I kept hoping it would click for me before the end.<br /><br />I will finish my review by saying, despite my criticisms, I was entertained throughout. So, even at those points when I was scratching my head at the aforementioned WTF-ness, I was still enjoying the book. This might seem a bit of an oxymoron - enjoying a book while at the same time not being satisfied - but, that is how it was!<br /><br />Note on audiobook - I don't recommend. I was not super impressed with the narrator and there is a character who cannot talk very well (to say any more would be a spoiler), so it is very hard to understand at times.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17559899830564871513" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1010666629" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1010666629" />
    <a title="Alejandro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/665584-alejandro"><img alt="Alejandro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1328672389p2/665584.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1010666629?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alejandro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alejandro" href="/user/show/665584-alejandro">Alejandro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=detective">detective</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1010666629" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16244529036742964701"><b>
  <u>He’s Hannibal alright, but she’s not Clarice!</u>
</b><br/><br/><br/><i>This is the third novel in the “Hannibal Lecter” book series.</i><br/><br/><br/><b>
  <u>WILL THE REAL MAIN CHARACTER PLEASE STAND UP?</u>
</b><br/><br/>I already explained in my review of <i>The Silence of the Lambs</i> that I became fan of the story for Clarice Starling instead of Dr. Hannibal Lecter, and pondering about the “ball effective time” of Lecter in the first two books, I still believe that Thomas Harris never thought that Hannibal Lecter would become the leading character in the book</span>
  <span id="freeText16244529036742964701" style="display:none"><b>
  <u>He’s Hannibal alright, but she’s not Clarice!</u>
</b><br /><br /><br /><i>This is the third novel in the “Hannibal Lecter” book series.</i><br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>WILL THE REAL MAIN CHARACTER PLEASE STAND UP?</u>
</b><br /><br />I already explained in my review of <i>The Silence of the Lambs</i> that I became fan of the story for Clarice Starling instead of Dr. Hannibal Lecter, and pondering about the “ball effective time” of Lecter in the first two books, I still believe that Thomas Harris never thought that Hannibal Lecter would become the leading character in the book series,…<br /><br />…but for better or worse, after the successful film, people embraced the character of Dr. Hannibal Lecter as the main one in the saga…<br /><br />…so it was logic now, that third book would be title <i>Hannibal</i>, so there wasn’t any doubt now that Lecter was the protagonist of the book series and the investigators Will Graham and Clarice Starling were just unlucky pawns of Dr. Lecter’ story.<br /><br />It was <b>HIS</b> story.<br /><br />Not Will’s, not Clarice’s, <b>HIS</b>.<br /><br />Will Graham had left the game. But Clarice could still make a second swing…<br /><br />…but Harris made the wrong call, in my humble opinion.<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>(NOT) UNDERSTANDING THE MONSTER</u>
</b><br /><br />In real life, monsters (because monsters exist) don’t need a reason to do what they do, and how they do it, sometimes it’s explained, but sometimes they are just…<br /><br />…monsters.<br /><br />However, in literature, there is a need of reason, a need of order, a need to avoid senseless chaos, if the monsters have a reason of what they do, how they do it,…<br /><br />…readers tend to accept the monsters. <br /><br />So, while the whole backstory of Hannibal Lecter would be deeply exposed in his following novel, <i>Hannibal Rising</i>, here, you learn the real crucial piece of information about the past of Lecter which gives you the key to understand the monster…<br /><br />…and while Harris seemed to develop the book’s story according to that…<br /><br />…at the end…<br /><br />…I don’t believe he understood Lecter, for not saying Starling neither.<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>(REALLY) KNOW YOUR CHARACTERS</u>
</b><br /><br />FBI Special Agent Clarice Starling was the star rookie thanks to catch “Buffalo Bill” even before of her formal graduation from Quantico, but…<br /><br />…that was seven years ago, and you can’t survive of only past glories, and she has just did an unspeakable mistake in a field assignment…<br /><br />…even Dr. Hannibal Lecter is worried about Clarice’s future.<br /><br />However, Dr. Lecter should be worried about himself since with a life doing so many wrong things to so many dangerous people, it was obvious that sooner or later, someone from his dark past would catch on him, looking for reckoning.<br /><br />Evil faces evil…<br /><br />…and Clarice’s mental health is in the middle.<br /><br />To be honest, two thirds of the book were quite well developed, showing many of the darkest things that human beings can do to other human beings…<br /><br />…it was the final act (don’t worry, I won’t spoil it) that I humbly think that Thomas Harris made the wrong call, <b>A VERY WRONG CALL</b>, with the characters of Hannibal and Clarice, not understanding the real reason of why they were so obssessed with each other…<br /><br />…and while you may think that only Clarice was ruined as character and that Hannibal wasn’t that bad ruined, trust me, if you understand Hannibal, even him was ruined to a level…<br /><br />…since even a monster can only fall to a certain degree, to avoid twisting its own code of why, what, how and to whom, do things.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16244529036742964701" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2188547273" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2188547273" />
    <a title="☘Misericordia☘ ~ The Serendipity Aegis ~  ⚡ϟ⚡ϟ⚡⛈ ✺❂❤❣" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6603759-misericordia-the-serendipity-aegis"><img alt="☘Misericordia☘ ~ The Serendipity Aegis ~  ⚡ϟ⚡ϟ⚡⛈ ✺❂❤❣" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585764390p2/6603759.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2188547273?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="☘Misericordia☘ ~ The Serendipity Aegis ~  ⚡ϟ⚡ϟ⚡⛈ ✺❂❤❣" class="user" itemprop="url" name="☘Misericordia☘ ~ The Serendipity Aegis ~  ⚡ϟ⚡ϟ⚡⛈ ✺❂❤❣" href="/user/show/6603759-misericordia-the-serendipity-aegis">☘Misericordia☘ ~ The Serendipity Aegis ~  ⚡ϟ⚡ϟ⚡⛈ ✺❂❤❣</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6603759-misericordia-the-serendipity-aegis?shelf=books-i-won-t-finish">books-i-won-t-finish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6603759-misericordia-the-serendipity-aegis?shelf=don-t-try-this-at-home">don-t-try-this-at-home</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6603759-misericordia-the-serendipity-aegis?shelf=shit">shit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2188547273" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7904435794705493359">DD 10/01/2017 Hated it passionately. This is that rare case where books are even worse than films, if you can believe it. <b>I don't know whatever possessed me to flip through this series.</b> Thankfully, that entity (demon of boredom? cluelesness?) was swiftly exorcised by pretty average writing, plot with Boeing-sized holes (were we really supposed to believe in vanishing special agents who almost no one conveniently gives a damn about?) and nauseating character-building. So far this is a DNF. Left t</span>
  <span id="freeText7904435794705493359" style="display:none">DD 10/01/2017 Hated it passionately. This is that rare case where books are even worse than films, if you can believe it. <b>I don't know whatever possessed me to flip through this series.</b> Thankfully, that entity (demon of boredom? cluelesness?) was swiftly exorcised by pretty average writing, plot with Boeing-sized holes (were we really supposed to believe in vanishing special agents who almost no one conveniently gives a damn about?) and nauseating character-building. So far this is a DNF. Left this unfinished and I don't think I'll ever return to it (of my own free will). Or maybe I will, let's live and see about it. <br /><br />This series felt a bit stupid, stilted, pretentious, even. It felt as if Lecter was made and MADE and freaking forced to look like an intellectual. And he didn't come across as one. Or maybe I'm judging intellect on a scale which includes the humanity factor, or lack thereof? Not sure about that.<br /><br />I didn't like the language. <br />The heroes felt without depth. <br />Or maybe I just don't like this concept due to severely disliking the TV snippets of this that have been irritating me to no end for ages. <br />The cannibal idea made me queasy. I can't fathom just how this stuff managed to give rise to that fan thing, where people would go on to even watch series on this topic. It'a goddamn mystery to me. The fact that our protagonist happens to be severely intellectual changes nothing for me. It doesn't add him any charm or any je-ne-sais-quoi or whatever it was that made this stuff popularish. Personally, I don't give a damn if a cannibal killer is an illuminating person or not. And a true intellectual? Don't think he was. I'm sure such an illuminated thinker might have found some other stuff to eat besides fellow humans, if only to be left alone by the society to pursue their oh-so-deep intellectual endeavours.<br /><br />The story with Clarice was, uh, nauseating. <b>How do you really craft a supposedly love story</b> (or whatever it was even supposed to look like!) <b>out of a story line with chemically assisted brainwashing???</b> That's what it truly was, things should be called their own names!!! And I don't really give a damn about Dr. Lecter's string theory equations (was that supposed to make him more likeable, him penning supposedly brilliant time physics while drugging Clarice out of her mind??). It does not make me sympathise with him, not at all.<br /><br />I'm not rating it so far because it feels worthy of a 1 measly star (for the writer's effort and wasted time, nothing else). Still, all those fans, they couldn't have been totally mistaken about this series. Or could they? I'll give it some time to sit with me. Maybe I missed something totally notable and earth-shattering about it and will find it someday. <s>.(Hopefully, that will not be that sad day my shrink goes to his one).</s>. <br /><br />At this point, <b>it's obvious to me that it was a mistake to read this. Note to self: I neet to be more scrupulous about choosing what I read.</b> Otherwise I'm going to be investing a lot more of my time into stuff I find distasteful!<br /><br />Q:<br />She was in the garden of the hurricane’s eye. (c)<br />Q:<br />She was awake and not awake. The bathroom was indeed comfortable and furnished with every amenity. In the following days she enjoyed long baths there, but she did not bother with her reflection in the mirror, so far was she from herself. (c)<br />Q:<br />“Mason is dead.”<br />“Ummmm,” Starling said. “Would you play for me?” (c)<br />Q:<br />Starling had no sense of time. Over the days and nights there were the conversations. She heard herself speaking for minutes on end, and she listened.<br />Sometimes she laughed at herself, hearing artless revelations that normally would have mortified her. The things she told Dr. Lecter were often surprising to her, sometimes distasteful to a normal sensibility, but what she said was always true. And Dr. Lecter spoke as well. In a low, even voice. He expressed interest and encouragement, but never surprise or censure. (c) <br />Q:<br />Sometimes they looked at a single bright object together to begin their talks, almost always there was but a single light source in the room. From day to day the bright object changed. (c) <b>Oh, yes, YES! The fact they they might have hypnotised each other, or gotten self-hypnotised together or whatever that was, is supposed to make this special, I'm sure. Kidding!</b><br />Q:<br />Dr. Lecter seemed to sense their arrival at an unexplored gallery in her mind. <u>Perhaps he heard trolls fighting on the other side of a wall.</u> (c) <b>Made me think of all those insufferable 'internal goddess' references in the 50 shades. Only here we get a gardenful of trolls instead! How unusual. </b><br />Q:<br />He replaced the teapot with a silver belt buckle.<br />“That’s my daddy’s,” Starling said. She clapped her hands together like a child.<br />“Yes,” Dr. Lecter said. “Clarice, would you like to talk with your father? Your father is here. Would you like to talk with him?”<br />“My daddy’s here! Hey! All right!” (c) <b>Vomit-inducing. This is exactly what I say when I see people so fascinated with all the shiny badges of merit, such as Doctor, Professor, President, etc. that they would miss what is right in front of them. This is a travesty of psychology. And 'Dr. Lecter' is no doctor,<br /> he might have been one at some point (or not!) but he is not one, after indulging in all his hobbies. </b><br />Q:<br />The monster settled back a micron in his chair. (c) <b> For once, a correct reference. </b><br />Q:<br /><s>Mr. Krendler is joining us for our first course.</s> (c) <b>Nasty! NASTY! I'm not going to give the detailed details here but they are extremely nasty. This is probably the worst thing I have ever read. Gross!</b> <br />Q:<br />Dr. Lecter and Clarice Starling often talk at dinner in languages other than Starling’s native English. She had college French and Spanish to build on, and she has found she has a good ear. They speak Italian a lot at meal-times; she finds a curious freedom in the visual nuances of the language. (c) <b>Once again, a pitiful attempt at either intellectuality or closeness: 'Oh, yeah, so they speak 3 foreign languages, they found each other, the intellectual soulmates, SQUEAAL!' For one thing, I don't think a couple of people with the depicted level of issues (to put it very mildly!) would be able or even want to get really close to each other. And there are lots of true polyglots out there, who have mastered a lot more languages and don't think it anything fancy. A very lame scene.</b><br />Q:<br />Their relationship has a great deal to do with the penetration of Clarice Starling, which she avidly welcomes and encourages...Sex is a splendid structure they add to every day. (c) <b>Uh-huh, of her own free will, of course, NOT.</b><br />Q:<br />It is hard to know what Starling remembers of the old life, what she chooses to keep. The drugs that held her in the first days have had no part in their lives for a long time. Nor the long talks with a single light source in the room. (c) <b>So, we are informed that the gal has been weaned off the drugs but is still on hypnosis. Good to know kidney failure might not be her next option. Still, I'm not really sure what purpose this achieves, ethic or aesthethic. Are we supposed to conclude at this point that hypnosis brainwashing is good for one's psyche?</b><br />Q:<br />We’ll withdraw now, while they are dancing on the terrace—the wise Barney has already left town and we must follow his example. For either of them to discover us would be fatal.<br />We can only learn so much and live. (c) <b>I can't help thinking it would have been best had I continued ignoring this series.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="7904435794705493359" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3281794334?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kerri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kerri" href="/user/show/51649609-kerri">Kerri</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51649609-kerri?shelf=kerri-s-favourites">kerri-s-favourites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer3281794334&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3281794334" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2342832539812316767">Please note my review also contains spoilers for DRACULA (I'm mainly referring to the 1992 film, 'Bram Stoker's Dracula', starring Gary Oldman and Winona Ryder, but it applies to any Dracula story, including the novel).<br/><br/>I found this to be the perfect ending to The Hannibal Lecter Trilogy. As soon as I finished it, I knew there would be a lot of people who would NOT be happy with the ending. That's fine, and I understand their points, but I loved it! <br/> This book is much more focused on Hannibal Lec</span>
  <span id="freeText2342832539812316767" style="display:none">Please note my review also contains spoilers for DRACULA (I'm mainly referring to the 1992 film, 'Bram Stoker's Dracula', starring Gary Oldman and Winona Ryder, but it applies to any Dracula story, including the novel).<br /><br />I found this to be the perfect ending to The Hannibal Lecter Trilogy. As soon as I finished it, I knew there would be a lot of people who would NOT be happy with the ending. That's fine, and I understand their points, but I loved it! <br /> This book is much more focused on Hannibal Lecter than the previous two - as the title implies. Clarice and Hannibal have almost been switched around here, in terms of the amount of time each is featured. Clarice is ever present in Hannibal's mind much as he was ever present in hers during the last book. Mason is a horrifying villain, and in the battle between him and Lecter, I was firmly rooting for Lecter. <br /> As I said, I loved the ending. It almost felt like a retelling of the conclusion of Dracula in some ways for me - a large part of me had always felt Mina and Dracula should have ended up together, a kind of twisted happy ever after. So of course, having Dr. Hannibal Lecter and Clarice Starling together was my ideal ending.<br /><br />This series as a whole is new favourite of mine - Perfect lockdown reading! 🐗</span>
  <a data-text-id="2342832539812316767" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="xhr_comment_body">

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer13144770520450471220">I have a theory about this horrible book.<br/><br/>Both <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28877.Red_Dragon__Hannibal_Lecter___1_' title='Red Dragon (Hannibal Lecter, #1) by Thomas Harris' rel='nofollow noopener'> Red Dragon</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23807.The_Silence_of_the_Lambs__Hannibal_Lecter___2_' title='The Silence of the Lambs (Hannibal Lecter, #2) by Thomas Harris' rel='nofollow noopener'> The Silence of the Lambs</a> are formidable pieces of pop fiction. They are well-written thrillers with great descriptions and characters. They were both adapted into great movies. They made <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12455.Thomas_Harris' title='Thomas Harris' rel='nofollow noopener'> Thomas Harris</a> a very rich man.<br/><br/>I think Mr. Harris made a bet, maybe with a friend or just to himself. He knew that his next novel would be snapped up for big bucks for the screen rights. He knew he would not get any control over the script. So he dec</span>
  <span id="freeText13144770520450471220" style="display:none">I have a theory about this horrible book.<br /><br />Both <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28877.Red_Dragon__Hannibal_Lecter___1_" title="Red Dragon (Hannibal Lecter, #1) by Thomas Harris" rel="nofollow noopener"> Red Dragon</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23807.The_Silence_of_the_Lambs__Hannibal_Lecter___2_" title="The Silence of the Lambs (Hannibal Lecter, #2) by Thomas Harris" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Silence of the Lambs</a> are formidable pieces of pop fiction. They are well-written thrillers with great descriptions and characters. They were both adapted into great movies. They made <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12455.Thomas_Harris" title="Thomas Harris" rel="nofollow noopener"> Thomas Harris</a> a very rich man.<br /><br />I think Mr. Harris made a bet, maybe with a friend or just to himself. He knew that his next novel would be snapped up for big bucks for the screen rights. He knew he would not get any control over the script. So he decided to write a book that would basically be un-filmable. It would be so preposterous, such dreck, that it would drive the screenwriters crazy. And Mr. Harris would be laughing all the way to the bank.<br /><br />This theory makes it possible to think that Thomas Harris is talented. There are other theories that eliminate that possibility. Of course, the publication of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32416.Hannibal_Rising__Hannibal_Lecter___4_" title="Hannibal Rising (Hannibal Lecter, #4) by Thomas Harris" rel="nofollow noopener"> Hannibal Rising</a> kind of shot my theory all to hell.<br /><br />I can't believe I was so excited about this book that I rushed out to buy it in hardcover. Ugh. I sold it to a used bookstore at the first opportunity.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2848130213096506474">Who knew The Hannibal Lecter series was a love story. <br/><br/>What Da Fuck did I just read? <br/><br/>I was hesitant to read Hannibal, various people told they hated the book and Everyone said they hated the movie and since I Loved both the book and movie versions of Red Dragon & The Silence of the Lambs...I was worried...Very worried.   <span id="freeText2848130213096506474" style="display:none">Who knew The Hannibal Lecter series was a love story. <br /><br />What Da Fuck did I just read? <br /><br />I was hesitant to read Hannibal, various people told they hated the book and Everyone said they hated the movie and since I Loved both the book and movie versions of Red Dragon &amp; The Silence of the Lambs...I was worried...Very worried. I waited over a month between finishing Lambs and starting Hannibal. I just kept putting it off &amp; putting it off until I finally decided to force myself to read it for my Read-A-Thon and I started reading it Friday &amp; finished on Sunday because despite the 544 page count Hannibal is a fast read. <br /><br />Hannibal picks up 6 or 7 years after the events of Lambs, Hannibal is in Italy living his best life as a fugitive. Clarice Starling is still at the FBI but her once promising career has stalled. Hannibal's only surviving victim has put a bounty out on him and he has the money to follow though with it. We finally start to get some background on Hannibal and we are shown a bit of Hannibal's psyche. The entire book is great but the last 100 pages are bat shit crazy and I'm not sure how I feel about it. The last 3 chapters were the most unsettling in the whole book and if you've read the book you know what I'm talking about. I'm undecided on if I'll watch the movie but probably yes. <br /><br />If you've started the Hannibal Lecter series I recommend reading Hannibal despite what you've heard about the movie.<br /><br />2018 Popsugar Reading Challenge: A Book About A Villain or Antihero.<br /><br />Around the year in 52 books: A book set in a country you'd like to visit but have never been to.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2037072206" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2037072206" />
    <a title="Tristan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3781470-tristan"><img alt="Tristan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601407992p2/3781470.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2037072206?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tristan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tristan" href="/user/show/3781470-tristan">Tristan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780434009404" href="/book/show/2336202.Hannibal">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2037072206" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14418058882362039574"><b>
  <i>“Did you ever think, Clarice, why the Philistines don't understand you? It's because you are the answer to Samson's riddle. You are the honey in the lion.”</i>
</b><br/><br/><br/>Clearly, the world didn’t need ( 'want' is another matter, of course ) one more novel featuring Hannibal Lecter. At the end of <i>Silence of the Lambs</i>, the good doctor had escaped the clutches of the law, after having treated the local authorities to a grotesque display of Grand Guignol theatre by way of parting gift. <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1500808585i/23385926._SX540_.png' width='400' height='214' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>This was a more than fit</span>
  <span id="freeText14418058882362039574" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>“Did you ever think, Clarice, why the Philistines don't understand you? It's because you are the answer to Samson's riddle. You are the honey in the lion.”</i>
</b><br><br><br>Clearly, the world didn’t need ( 'want' is another matter, of course ) one more novel featuring Hannibal Lecter. At the end of <i>Silence of the Lambs</i>, the good doctor had escaped the clutches of the law, after having treated the local authorities to a grotesque display of Grand Guignol theatre by way of parting gift. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1500808585i/23385926._SX540_.png" width="400" height="214" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This was a more than fitting, infinitely memorable <i>adieu</i> to the cannibal, who from his first appearance in <i>Red Dragon</i> never was supposed to be a main character, let alone the protagonist. But of course Harris - for a variety of reasons, financial considerations probably being the main one - couldn’t resist revisiting Lecter, and 11 years after <i>Silence of the Lambs</i>, <i>Hannibal</i> arrived, which places Lecter prominently in the foreground, now living in Florence as a museum curator under the alias of Dr. Fell.<br><br>As can be expected, the mere fact of him being no longer confined significantly diminishes the interest the character previously managed to pique. Locked up, with only his intellect and ingenuity at his disposal to manipulate whatever unfortunate soul he deems worthy, he represented a much more intriguing, insidious creature. One only has to recall that one time he managed to talk a fellow inmate into biting off and swallowing his tongue just by whispering to him at night. What those words were exactly, no one knows, and that’s precisely what makes it disturbing.<br> <br>Oddly, Harris in a rare moment of authorial commentary, almost inadvertently intimates this fact in <i>Hannibal</i>: <br><br> <blockquote>
  <i>“Dr. Lecter stood at a distance from her, very still, as he had stood in his cell when she first saw him. We are accustomed to seeing him unfettered now. It is not shocking to see him in open space with another mortal creature.”</i>
  <br>
</blockquote><br>Not shocking, you say? Thank you for proving my point for me, Mr. Harris. <br><br>My previous “issue” with <i>Red Dragon</i> and <i>Silence of the Lambs</i> consisted of there being not much new material in those books, since they both had been rather literally adapted (in the case of <i>Red Dragon</i>, twice) with few deviations. The problem rested with me, not with the books themselves. <br><br>With <i>Hannibal</i> though, you get quite a different animal, which initially delighted me. There is a substantial subplot involving Mason’s peculiar sister, Hannibal’s traumatic memory of the tragic fate of his sister Mischa plays a considerable role, and Ardelia Mapp and Jack Crawford (both excised from the film) have been carried over from <i>Silence of the Lambs</i>, which make for wonderful additions to the plot, even though my reservations about a freely operating Lecter were still very much present. Yet, for being an otherwise accomplished, even highly enjoyable thriller, <i>Hannibal</i> unfortunately ends with a callous betrayal. <br><br>Not of the kind perpetrated by one fictional character to another mind you, but by the author to his audience. As endings go, it surely must go down in history as one of the most ill-advised and ignominious. Normally I am a passionate advocate for the idea of the creator’s absolute sovereignty, who is under no obligation whatsoever to accommodate his audience. Yet here both Harris’ lack of judgment and the unwillingness (cowardice?) of his editor to stand firm and demand the ending to be replaced simply must be deplored. <br><br>A mere 20 pages. That’s all it took for Harris to destroy the essence of Clarice Starling, one of the best loved female characters in all of fiction, only in order to ham-fistedly drive home the theme of mutual attraction between her and Lecter. Granted, this dynamic between the two was always there, lurking beneath the surface, but the impossibility of it ever materializing was exactly what made it interesting. Yet <i>Hannibal</i> ends as a twisted love story, fully consummated, which Harris apparently feels the need to make explicit in detail:<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>"Their relationship has a great deal to do with the penetration of Clarice Starling, which she avidly welcomes and encourages."</i>
</blockquote><br>Yes, it actually says that. It's not so much the exploration of Starling as a sexual being that is irksome here (previously, she was almost solely focussed on her career, with not much thought given to romantic interactions with men) but how she is just undergoing the process, as if she has no agency. This is a radical departure from the individual established in <i>Silence of the Lambs</i> who, even in her inexperience as a rookie, very much had a mind of her own. That one line is absolutely devastating to this character.<br> <br>Starling was intrigued by Lecter to be sure, but she wasn’t as foolish as to think some healthy relationship could ever be maintained with him, an amoral cannibal. I didn’t buy it, even if she was initially under the influence of drugs and hypnosis. It’s clear in later passages she isn’t anymore under that influence, but actually chooses this life, with all her mental faculties intact. It totally goes against all we had come to learn about her. Above all else, Starling is strong-willed, highly intelligent, determined and has a rock-solid moral compass. <br><br>It’s incredible that Harris didn’t realize those were the exact qualities that made her so popular in the first place. Ultimately, she just ends up as Lecter’s plaything, a puppet of his own creation. It really makes one wonder what went on in the author’s head at the time. Was he under time restraint, his deadline fast approaching? I'd really love to know the answer to that one. <br><br><i>Hannibal</i> is far from being a bad book, and I suggest you do read it, but go in with expectations tempered. With the ending being what it is, I can’t possibly give it more than two stars. Just goes to show that even if the first 500 or so pages were good and some passages even quite excellent, the whole enterprise can be ruined by the subsequent twenty. <br><br>It's one of the most delicious of ironies that Thomas Harris - the creator of a famous fictional cannibal - would end up cannibalizing his own work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14418058882362039574" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2037072206">

<div id="review_205023629" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/205023629" />
    <a title="Stargirl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6197685-stargirl"><img alt="Stargirl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349173929p2/6197685.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/205023629?book_show_action=true">Sep 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stargirl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stargirl" href="/user/show/6197685-stargirl">Stargirl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer205023629" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10925168181207584934">Oh that ending. Sublime. <br/><br/>Gothic horror detective chase story, blends genres with great ease, affirming Harris as a master storyteller- as if we didn't already know. Don't go in expecting another Red Dragon or Silence of the Lambs. This is a much bigger, far darker experience. It's all in the title people. Silence was the story of Clarice Starling, so there is a lot of hope and innocence to it. This one, is about the dark side of her character- and of that awful/awesome Doctor Mr Lecter himself. </span>
  <span id="freeText10925168181207584934" style="display:none">Oh that ending. Sublime. <br /><br />Gothic horror detective chase story, blends genres with great ease, affirming Harris as a master storyteller- as if we didn't already know. Don't go in expecting another Red Dragon or Silence of the Lambs. This is a much bigger, far darker experience. It's all in the title people. Silence was the story of Clarice Starling, so there is a lot of hope and innocence to it. This one, is about the dark side of her character- and of that awful/awesome Doctor Mr Lecter himself. <br /><br />A decadent spiral into madness and obliteration. A glimpse into hell. <br /><br />The quality of the writing is of the highest order, blurring the lines between popular fiction and literature. <br /><br />Ingenious and over the top, a true sequel that shows no mercy. <br /><br />And the ending... Sweet God. It really puts the reader in his/her place, reminding you that you have no control over the characters you read. The author is in charge and he will take you into places, dragging and screaming, that you don't want to know. Harris is James Gumb from Silence of the Lambs and this book is the pit he threw Catherine Martin into. Only she escaped . . . </span>

<div id="review_75719188" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75719188" />
    <a title="Sufferingbruin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2874565-sufferingbruin"><img alt="Sufferingbruin" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/75719188?book_show_action=true">Oct 25, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sufferingbruin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sufferingbruin" href="/user/show/2874565-sufferingbruin">Sufferingbruin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer75719188" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17923522025577052333">Lord, what an awful book. Awash in mediocrity from first page to last. It has mediocre characters (the same which were so captivating in "Silence of the Lambs"), mediocre dialogue, mediocre scenery, virtually no suspense (but a plethora of pointlessly putrid acts), and a meandering narrative that often lacks consistency of time and place. "Hannibal" does not induce fear or revulsion so much as groans and guffaws. But don't worry: there's a bleeding HIV-postiive woman holding a baby whose last li</span>
  <span id="freeText17923522025577052333" style="display:none">Lord, what an awful book. Awash in mediocrity from first page to last. It has mediocre characters (the same which were so captivating in "Silence of the Lambs"), mediocre dialogue, mediocre scenery, virtually no suspense (but a plethora of pointlessly putrid acts), and a meandering narrative that often lacks consistency of time and place. "Hannibal" does not induce fear or revulsion so much as groans and guffaws. But don't worry: there's a bleeding HIV-postiive woman holding a baby whose last line is "let's swap fluids, bitch" before she's shot to death mid-crime. There are man eating pigs who are intended to be filmed in the act by people from the porn industry, at the behest of a sub-villain recovering from having his face chewed up by dogs. Thankfully, he gets by with having the tears of children put into his IV. No, that last sentence was not a joke. And of course, our two rivals are back.<br /><br />Clarice Starling gets the worst of it in "Hannibal". She has lost all trace of vulnerability and trepidation so there's nothing to overcome. In other words, she's lost what made her human in the previous book. Here, she's wizened super-woman; so cynical, so powerful and of course, distant. We don't know why she is all of these things and Harris doesn't seem to care. There are hints of being passed over at the agency and we can guess from her dour persona that Special Agent Starling is weary of the world but these are only guesses; nothing Clarice says or does leads to inferences one way or the other because it's her turn to play second fiddle. <br /><br />Harris' previous efforts, "Red Dragon" and especially "Silence of the Lambs" are both terrific. In both, Lecter plays a role (small in "Dragon" and of course, much larger in "Lambs") but supporting roles. "Red Dragon" explored profiling, a valuable but punishing trade. How much damage can happen when you take on the mind of a killer to catch the killer? Will Graham finds out in a brief visit to Lecter, ostensibly to get information on another serial killer when Lecter memorably calls him out: "You came back to get the smell back, didn't you? Smell yourself." Harris went further with "Lambs", with a myriad of themes, all of them intriguing, all of them meticulously developed. Which brings us to "Hannibal" and a crucial question after a painful week of reading: What is the theme here? Lecter is the unquestioned star of the show but what does Lecter have to tell us? I'm at a loss. We know he insists on only the best material accoutrements (to borrow a phrase from our sociopath)--the best food, the best cars and for heaven's sake, the best clothes. He travels the world, sniffing at the coarseness of his fellow citizens when he's not killing them. So Harris, in his infinite wisdom, has made Lecter a snob whose only relief from the utter pain of flying coach (oh, the humanity) is the foie de gras he sneaks on board. <br /><br />It takes awhile but the two engage. By that time, the reader is either beaten into submission or baffled as to why another page is to be turned. If--and it's a big if--reading continues, there is a conclusion so laugh-out-loud ridiculous one has to wonder about Harris' true intent. The best that can be said is that he wants to wash his hands of the whole enterprise. If so, mission accomplished. There is literally nowhere left for either to go, no worlds left to conquer though the world they inhabit doesn't resemble anything like the one we are living in. <br /><br />Shorter review: this is a book that makes you wish a zero star rating was possible. <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17923522025577052333" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText18087806073050328392" style="display:none">Jesh, I have to say it. Cover your ears if you can’t handle profanity.<br /><br />But for fucks sake was that ending?!!<br />Because of the way <b>Hannibal</b> ended, I can’t rate this higher then <b>2.5 STARS</b>.<br /><br /><b>Hannibal</b> almost put me into a book slump. I’m not lying. I’ve read <b>Red Dragon</b> and <b>The Silence of the Lambs</b> and loved both. I couldn’t put either book down and was obsessed.<br /><br />With <b>Hannibal</b>, I didn’t even want to pick it up at times. Thank goodness I read other books at the same time and the dreaded book slump didn’t get me!<br /><br />Almost all the characters in this book are abhorrent, evil and vile. I need to take a hot shower now to clean off the gross.<br />I’ve never wanted two characters more in a book to die.<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />Mason Verger and Paul Krendler.<br />In my opinion, I liked how poetic it was that Verger died by his sister that he abused. Maybe he should have just cut the end?! hahaha *long sigh*<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />I really did not like Lecter and Clarice’s relationship at the end. It’s a love relationship now and she’s going to throw everything away that meant anything to her, like Ardelia Mapp and being an FBI investigator?!<br /><br />Really?! I’ve got serious eye roll going on. I just can’t believe this and go with such a huge character transformation and change.<br />It’s too unbelievable for me. It changed the whole complicated nature of their relationship in my opinion. I much prefer hunter and hunted between them. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Am I glad I read it? I guess. I’m glad I read it with friends and had moral support to finish it!<br />In my opinion, just stick with <b>Red Dragon</b> and <b>The Silence of the Lambs</b>. You'll thank me later.</span>

<div id="review_253714812" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/253714812" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1256603-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596206765p2/1256603.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/253714812?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/1256603-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1256603-sara?shelf=girl-power">girl-power</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1256603-sara?shelf=polarizing">polarizing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1256603-sara?shelf=classic-thriller">classic-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1256603-sara?shelf=weird-romance">weird-romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1256603-sara?shelf=matthew-is-a-pain">matthew-is-a-pain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1256603-sara?shelf=yum-brains">yum-brains</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1256603-sara?shelf=atmospheric">atmospheric</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer253714812" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2319464442710843860">In the interest of full disclosure blah blah Hannibal is my aesthetic yadda yadda Mads Mikkelsen herp de derp cheekbones blah blah darkness is sexy. <br/><br/>In all seriousness if you’re reading this I’m going to assume that 1.) You’ve got at least a working knowledge of Hannibal Lecter and his adventures and also possibly 2.) You’re a friend of Matthew’s and you think its just HILARIOUS that he makes me write these. <br/><br/>Incidentally Matthew I hate you. Look I made a shelf about it!<br/><br/>I like this book. I would,</span>
  <span id="freeText2319464442710843860" style="display:none">In the interest of full disclosure blah blah Hannibal is my aesthetic yadda yadda Mads Mikkelsen herp de derp cheekbones blah blah darkness is sexy. <br /><br />In all seriousness if you’re reading this I’m going to assume that 1.) You’ve got at least a working knowledge of Hannibal Lecter and his adventures and also possibly 2.) You’re a friend of Matthew’s and you think its just HILARIOUS that he makes me write these. <br /><br />Incidentally Matthew I hate you. Look I made a shelf about it!<br /><br />I like this book. I would, in fact, go so far as to say this is my second favorite Hannibal Lecter book after <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28877.Red_Dragon__Hannibal_Lecter___1_" title="Red Dragon (Hannibal Lecter, #1) by Thomas Harris" rel="nofollow noopener">Red Dragon</a>. Many fans of Dr. Lecter will disagree violently with me but to me this book and its ending is the only possible way that Dr. Lecter and Clarise Starling's story could have ended.<br /><br />For the sake of everyone's sanity (no one wants to hear me talk about cheekbones and Danish accents trust me we’d be here all night) I'll be talking strictly about the books here though I'd be lying if I said my appreciation for the books hasn't been colored by the television show. Cause it has.<br /><br />To refresh your memory and because I am aware that not everyone is quite as addicted to this character as I am and thus does not retain practically perfect recall of every part of his story let me remind you that when we last left the erudite, dashing, brilliant, cannibalistic Dr. Lecter he was assuring FBI trainee Clarice Starling that while her solving of the kidnapping of Catherine Martin and the subsequent killing of Buffalo Bill might "silence the screaming of the lambs" for her for the moment, that silence would not last forever. He reminded Clarice that, having escaped custody by taking a leaf out of Leatherface's book, he had "no plans to call on you" finding the world more interesting with her in it.<br /><br />I'm probably blending the movie and the book a bit there but I do so love that line. If you know Hannibal at all you know the worst thing he can be is bored. His MO isn't so much murder as it is manipulation of the people around him just to see what will happen. Sometimes that includes murder, of course, but in the end his needs seem much more wrapped up in living, breathing people and how they move through the world. He has no empathy so he's totally unfettered by all the societal conventions and feelings that keep the rest of us from acting on all of our impulses, good and bad, and he takes full advantage. As I've said before part of my attraction to him resides in wishing I could live the same way. To a degree. Of course.<br /><br />He's also always been on a quest for someone to share that life with. At least that's how I've interpreted it. Thomas Harris has never been especially forthcoming about the relationship Hannibal (I like to think we'd be on a first name basis if he were real) had with his first "protege" Will Graham, the FBI profiler gifted with such an intense sense of empathy it essentially drove him mad, but I see that relationship as something of a high school romance. It was fraught with high emotion and passion with Graham constantly fighting his worse impulses and Hannibal doing his damndest to bring them out. It's that first relationship we all go through that's all wrong and dramatic and sort of sets the tone for everything that comes after.<br /><br />Clarice Starling is Hannibal's after.<br /><br />She's sort of a Will Graham 2.0. Gifted with the same intense intelligence but not handicapped by Graham's affinity for evil. She knows right from wrong, its the core of her existence, its what's taken her all the way to the FBI and who knows what she might have gone on to do had she not walked into Hannibal's sights. <br /><br />They dance a beautiful dance around each other in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23807.The_Silence_of_the_Lambs__Hannibal_Lecter___2_" title="The Silence of the Lambs (Hannibal Lecter, #2) by Thomas Harris" rel="nofollow noopener">The Silence of the Lambs</a>, like a slow burning romance, a really fucked up one but still a romance of sorts. Harris never says a blessed word about attraction or desire, and the only time its mentioned is when one of them is pointedly denying it exists. <br /><br />But they do seem to require one another. Because when <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32418.Hannibal__Hannibal_Lecter___3_" title="Hannibal (Hannibal Lecter, #3) by Thomas Harris" rel="nofollow noopener">Hannibal</a> begins they are both of them adrift. Clarice's career with the FBI has stalled after a disastrous case and an unfortunate personal issue with a superior. Hannibal is spinning his wheels in Florence. He's got his view of the Duomo from the Belvedere and after some fancy footwork a job in the art world but its all so much window dressing, there's nothing for him to...sink his teeth into (sorry couldn't help it). <br /><br />Yes, the novel stalls out a bit while Hannibal galavants around Florence murdering people, and buying Clarice perfume, and baffling a bumbling Italian detective. That story goes on so long it might as well be its own novel. But, there's an interesting third player added to the mix who soon changes the game. A good old fashioned Thomas Harris nasty.<br /><br />Mason Verger is just disgusting. He's not even attractively disgusting like Buffalo Bill or sympathetically disgusting like Francis Dolarhyde, he's just plain disgusting. A former patient of Lecter's, he's now intent on capturing Lecter and paying him back for a drug fueled night of barbaric debauchery that left him in a wheelchair (among other things). When Verger's agents attempt to capture Lecter he senses that Starling is tied up in this somehow and despite the risk, or perhaps because of it, he returns to the US to find her.<br /><br />Wackiness ensues!<br /><br />Is it a touch melodramatic? Well its a book about a cannibal genius who's in love with an FBI agent and trying to elude a guy with no face who wants to feed him to pigs so you tell me. <br /><br />Is there something a little weird and icky about trying to actually make Lecter a hero by putting him up against someone who kind of deserved all the terrible stuff Lecter did to him? Yeah it kind of dulls Lecter’s claws a bit.<br /><br />Is it uncomfortably homophobic at times? Yes, yes it is. Mason Verger has a lesbian sister who is like your grandfather's concept of what a lesbian is. So she's addicted to steroids, ripped harder then Rambo, and fond of crew cuts and, according to Harris, at least partly into women because she was molested as a child. <br /><br />Did I have a weird urge to find out what brains taste like after reading it? I'm not answering that.<br /><br />But of course whatever you thought about the book as a whole, the real issue for everyone is the ending. Everyone always loses their damn minds because <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[they’re boning in Argentina by the end. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Here's what I think. It just makes sense. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[There was something horrifying and thrilling in watching Hannibal “rescue” Clarice from the madness of the Verger estate. Its oddly romantic and dreamy when Clarice wakes up in that beautiful bed to a closet full of designer clothes and to memories of strange, drug addled hypnotic suggestions, memories of a reunion with her dead father. Finding out that he intended to make her the living vessel for his long dead sister Mischa made a horrible kind of sense. Mischa and her death are the foundation on which Lecter's life was built. In trying to correct the error of her death he was attempting to correct the course of his own life. Clarice's refusal to accept that, to accept Hannibal's own metaphoric death and instead offer him the one thing he's always wanted, someone to share himself (the psychotic cannibal) with is the only way to save him. In a weirdly beautiful moment I have never forgotten she offers her naked breast to him, reminding him that unlike when he surrendered his mother's breast to Mischa when she was born he won't have to do the same with her. Then Harris writes one of my all time favorite lines:<br /><br />"He came swiftly from his chair to her, went on a knee before her chair, and bent to her coral and cream in the firelight his dark sleek head." <br /><br />Lecter is a monster without a doubt. He’s so far beyond anything diagnosable that they don’t even have a name for what he is. He is perfect, unrepentant evil. He has no place in the world. But in a weird way neither does Clarice. The world she wants to be a part of can’t accept her. She’s too beautiful, too smart, too noble for the mire of corruption that is the FBI and those who control it. Incorruptible goodness. That world will kill her before it allows her to join it. So why shouldn’t they build a new world of their own together? There’s beauty and I think possibly even love in this strange dark life they forge together and I can’t help but love it. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The world is just a more interesting place with these two in it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2319464442710843860" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_510502630" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/510502630" />
    <a title="Chris Shepherdson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16182382-chris-shepherdson"><img alt="Chris Shepherdson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1375618518p2/16182382.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/510502630?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Shepherdson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Shepherdson" href="/user/show/16182382-chris-shepherdson">Chris Shepherdson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer510502630" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2360159502492511698">When Thomas Harris created the infamous Hannibal Lector in Red Dragon he couldn't have known the influence that character would have on crime fiction for the next decade. By the time he came to write Hannibal, ten years after his previous book, The Silence of The Lambs, he must have felt some serious pressure. The fact that Hannibal is the book it is, when written under these circumstances, makes it all the more remarkable.<br/>To call it a crime novel is doing it a grave injustice and reading it as </span>
  <span id="freeText2360159502492511698" style="display:none">When Thomas Harris created the infamous Hannibal Lector in Red Dragon he couldn't have known the influence that character would have on crime fiction for the next decade. By the time he came to write Hannibal, ten years after his previous book, The Silence of The Lambs, he must have felt some serious pressure. The fact that Hannibal is the book it is, when written under these circumstances, makes it all the more remarkable.<br />To call it a crime novel is doing it a grave injustice and reading it as such will also leave the reader disappointed. This book sits closer to Stoker's Dracula or Shelly's Frankenstein than the serial killer fare of Michael Connelly. Treat this book more as gothic fairytale and you won't be disappointed. <br />The greatest triumph of The Silence of The Lambs is, as iconic as the film version is, it scarecely registers as you read the book. You are taken into another world and any thought of Jodie Foster and Anthony Hopkins are gone. The same is true of Hannibal, and then some.</span>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText2065313225693889548" style="display:none">This is the third instalment in the Hannibal Lecter series.<br /><br />I might have put this book down for a whole month but then I binged through over half of it in a single day, so make of that what you will. <br /><br />I have a difficult time saying I 'enjoyed' this book, given the dark topics it focused on... but that was exactly my concluding reaction. Harris takes the readers across the globe and through a multitude of perspectives to deliver a fully-fleshed examination of Hannibal Lecter's return to the forefront of the FBIs attention. It kept me guessing and grossed out, and the insights were all brief which also ensured I kept the pages turning.</span>
